<!-- COUNTRY: AFG | Afghanistan -->

# Afghanistan

## Summary Assessment

Afghanistan is classified in the packet as a Low income economy in Middle East, North Africa, Afghanistan & Pakistan, with the latest observation in 2020. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 20.0 and a low status. The latest reserve position is US$9.7 billion, providing 16.2 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 22.40x. The current account shows a deficit of 15.7% of GDP, equal to -US$3.1 billion. The reported baseline liquidity gap is -21.9% of GDP, or -US$4.4 billion. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2020, reported reserves were US$9.7 billion, compared with annual imports of US$7.2 billion and GDP of US$20.0 billion. Import cover stood at 16.2 months. On the packet's scoring framework, this import-cover position contributes no risk to the overall vulnerability score. Short-term external debt was US$435.1 million, and reserves covered that stock by 22.40x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at -US$4.4 billion, or -21.9% of GDP, which indicates a reserve cushion under the packet's baseline liquidity metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 15.7% of GDP, equivalent to -US$3.1 billion. That deficit is the main flow-side pressure because it implies that the economy's external payments exceeded external receipts on the current-account measure provided in the packet. The current-account component carries a risk score of 100.0, making it an important contributor to the overall dashboard reading. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$9.7 billion, while the current-account balance is -US$3.1 billion. Imports of US$7.2 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$435.1 million adds a stock-related liquidity test. The negative baseline gap indicates that reserves exceed the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The packet provides only one annual observation in the recent-history series, so trend interpretation is necessarily limited. In that observation, import cover was 16.2 months, reserves covered short-term external debt by 22.40 times, the current account was -15.7% of GDP, and the baseline liquidity gap was -21.9% of GDP. Because no multi-year sequence is available inside the packet, the note should be read as a point-in-time liquidity assessment rather than an assessment of a demonstrated trend.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 20.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 53 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is low vulnerability, indicating that the composite measure does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 100.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field contains only one year, so the available public-data indicators are incomplete for evaluating persistence, volatility, or turning points. The latest observation is 2020, so the available public-data indicators are incomplete for assessing developments after that year. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.

<!-- COUNTRY: AGO | Angola -->

# Angola

## Summary Assessment

Angola is classified in the packet as a Lower middle income economy in Sub-Saharan Africa, with the latest observation in 2024. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 0.0 and a low status. The latest reserve position is US$14.2 billion, providing 8.7 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 2.45x. The current account shows a surplus of 6.2% of GDP, equal to US$6.3 billion. The reported baseline liquidity gap is -3.5% of GDP, or -US$3.5 billion. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$14.2 billion, compared with annual imports of US$19.6 billion and GDP of US$101.0 billion. Import cover stood at 8.7 months. On the packet's scoring framework, this import-cover position contributes no risk to the overall vulnerability score. Short-term external debt was US$5.8 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 2.45x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at -US$3.5 billion, or -3.5% of GDP, which indicates a reserve cushion under the packet's baseline liquidity metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a surplus of 6.2% of GDP, equivalent to US$6.3 billion. That surplus is supportive for external liquidity because it adds a flow-side source of foreign exchange rather than creating an immediate financing need on this measure. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$14.2 billion, while the current-account balance is US$6.3 billion. Imports of US$19.6 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$5.8 billion adds a stock-related liquidity test. The negative baseline gap indicates that reserves exceed the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover fell from 12.3 months to 8.7 months. This points to a weaker position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 6.2 months to 12.3 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt fell from 4.7x to 2.5x. This points to a weaker position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 2.5x to 6.3x. The current-account balance rose from -0.8% to 6.2%. This points to a stronger position. The current account ranged from -0.8% to 10.6% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap rose from -10.5% of GDP to -3.5% of GDP. This points to some tightening of the liquidity position because a higher gap is less comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -11.1% of GDP to -2.2% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 0.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 76 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is low vulnerability, indicating that the composite measure does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.

# Albania

## Summary Assessment

Albania is classified in the packet as an Upper middle income economy in Europe & Central Asia, with the latest observation in 2024. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 10.0 and a low status. The latest reserve position is US$6.5 billion, providing 6.7 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 5.25x. The current account shows a deficit of 2.4% of GDP, equal to -US$647.9 million. The reported baseline liquidity gap is -6.3% of GDP, or -US$1.7 billion. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$6.5 billion, compared with annual imports of US$11.7 billion and GDP of US$27.0 billion. Import cover stood at 6.7 months. On the packet's scoring framework, this import-cover position contributes no risk to the overall vulnerability score. Short-term external debt was US$1.2 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 5.25x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at -US$1.7 billion, or -6.3% of GDP, which indicates a reserve cushion under the packet's baseline liquidity metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 2.4% of GDP, equivalent to -US$647.9 million. That deficit is the main flow-side pressure because it implies that the economy's external payments exceeded external receipts on the current-account measure provided in the packet. The current-account component carries a risk score of 50.0, making it an important contributor to the overall dashboard reading. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$6.5 billion, while the current-account balance is -US$647.9 million. Imports of US$11.7 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$1.2 billion adds a stock-related liquidity test. The negative baseline gap indicates that reserves exceed the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover fell from 7.1 months to 6.7 months. This points to a weaker position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 6.5 months to 10.3 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt rose from 1.7x to 5.2x. This points to a stronger position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 1.7x to 5.4x. The current-account balance rose from -7.4% to -2.4%. This points to a stronger position. The current account ranged from -8.6% to -1.7% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap fell from 8.0% of GDP to -6.3% of GDP. This points to some easing of pressure because a lower gap is more comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -9.7% of GDP to 8.0% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 10.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 66 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is low vulnerability, indicating that the composite measure does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 50.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. # Argentina

## Summary Assessment

Argentina is classified in the packet as an Upper middle income economy in Latin America & Caribbean, with the latest observation in 2024. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 60.0 and a high status. The latest reserve position is US$29.6 billion, providing 4.4 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 0.61x. The current account shows a surplus of 0.9% of GDP, equal to US$5.7 billion. The reported baseline liquidity gap is 6.2% of GDP, or US$39.3 billion. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$29.6 billion, compared with annual imports of US$80.9 billion and GDP of US$638.4 billion. Import cover stood at 4.4 months. On the packet's scoring framework, the import-cover position carries a risk score of 50.0, indicating that the stock of reserves is not fully comfortable against the import bill. Short-term external debt was US$48.6 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 0.61x. The short-term-debt coverage component has a risk score of 100.0, so debt-service concentration at the short end remains a relevant liquidity issue in the dashboard. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$39.3 billion, or 6.2% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a surplus of 0.9% of GDP, equivalent to US$5.7 billion. That surplus is supportive for external liquidity because it adds a flow-side source of foreign exchange rather than creating an immediate financing need on this measure. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$29.6 billion, while the current-account balance is US$5.7 billion. Imports of US$80.9 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$48.6 billion adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover fell from 7.4 months to 4.4 months. This points to a weaker position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 3.1 months to 9.3 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt fell from 1.0x to 0.6x. This points to a weaker position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 0.4x to 1.0x. The current-account balance rose from -4.8% to 0.9%. This points to a stronger position. The current account ranged from -5.2% to 1.4% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap fell from 8.4% of GDP to 6.2% of GDP. This points to some easing of pressure because a lower gap is more comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from 4.2% of GDP to 11.6% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 60.0, with a high status and a vulnerability rank of 9 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is high vulnerability, so the country sits in the more pressured segment of the sample on the packet's composite measure. The component scores are 50.0 for import-cover risk, 100.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.

# Armenia

## Summary Assessment

Armenia is classified in the packet as an Upper middle income economy in Europe & Central Asia, with the latest observation in 2024. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 70.0 and a high status. The latest reserve position is US$3.7 billion, providing 2.2 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 1.05x. The current account shows a deficit of 4.6% of GDP, equal to -US$1.2 billion. The reported baseline liquidity gap is 18.1% of GDP, or US$4.7 billion. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$3.7 billion, compared with annual imports of US$19.8 billion and GDP of US$26.0 billion. Import cover stood at 2.2 months. On the packet's scoring framework, the import-cover position carries a risk score of 100.0, indicating that the stock of reserves is not fully comfortable against the import bill. Short-term external debt was US$3.5 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 1.05x. The short-term-debt coverage component has a risk score of 50.0, so debt-service concentration at the short end remains a relevant liquidity issue in the dashboard. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$4.7 billion, or 18.1% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 4.6% of GDP, equivalent to -US$1.2 billion. That deficit is the main flow-side pressure because it implies that the economy's external payments exceeded external receipts on the current-account measure provided in the packet. The current-account component carries a risk score of 50.0, making it an important contributor to the overall dashboard reading. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

Reserve coverage of short-term external debt fell from 2.6x to 1.1x. This points to a weaker position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 1.1x to 2.6x. The current-account balance fell from -1.3% to -4.6%. This points to a weaker position. The current account ranged from -7.2% to 0.7% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap rose from 1.1% of GDP to 18.1% of GDP. This points to some tightening of the liquidity position because a higher gap is less comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from 1.1% of GDP to 18.1% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 70.0, with a high status and a vulnerability rank of 7 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is high vulnerability, so the country sits in the more pressured segment of the sample on the packet's composite measure. The component scores are 100.0 for import-cover risk, 50.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 50.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

# Azerbaijan

## Summary Assessment

Azerbaijan is classified in the packet as an Upper middle income economy in Europe & Central Asia, with the latest observation in 2024. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 0.0 and a low status. The latest reserve position is US$12.7 billion, providing 5.6 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 58.14x. The current account shows a surplus of 6.3% of GDP, equal to US$4.7 billion. The reported baseline liquidity gap is -7.6% of GDP, or -US$5.6 billion. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$12.7 billion, compared with annual imports of US$27.3 billion and GDP of US$74.3 billion. Import cover stood at 5.6 months. On the packet's scoring framework, this import-cover position contributes no risk to the overall vulnerability score. Short-term external debt was US$218.4 million, and reserves covered that stock by 58.14x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at -US$5.6 billion, or -7.6% of GDP, which indicates a reserve cushion under the packet's baseline liquidity metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a surplus of 6.3% of GDP, equivalent to US$4.7 billion. That surplus is supportive for external liquidity because it adds a flow-side source of foreign exchange rather than creating an immediate financing need on this measure. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$12.7 billion, while the current-account balance is US$4.7 billion. Imports of US$27.3 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$218.4 million adds a stock-related liquidity test. The negative baseline gap indicates that reserves exceed the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover rose from 4.9 months to 5.6 months. This points to a stronger position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 4.5 months to 6.6 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt rose from 10.4x to 58.1x. This points to a stronger position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 10.4x to infx. The current-account balance rose from 4.1% to 6.3%. This points to a stronger position. The current account ranged from -0.7% to 29.8% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap fell from -5.1% of GDP to -7.6% of GDP. This points to some easing of pressure because a lower gap is more comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -9.5% of GDP to -3.6% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 0.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 81 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is low vulnerability, indicating that the composite measure does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.

<!-- COUNTRY: BGD | Bangladesh -->

# Bangladesh

## Summary Assessment

Bangladesh is classified in the packet as a Lower middle income economy in South Asia, with the latest observation in 2024. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 20.0 and a low status. The latest reserve position is US$21.4 billion, providing 3.5 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 1.65x. The current account shows a deficit of 0.7% of GDP, equal to -US$3.0 billion. The reported baseline liquidity gap is 2.9% of GDP, or US$12.9 billion. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$21.4 billion, compared with annual imports of US$73.4 billion and GDP of US$450.1 billion. Import cover stood at 3.5 months. On the packet's scoring framework, the import-cover position carries a risk score of 50.0, indicating that the stock of reserves is not fully comfortable against the import bill. Short-term external debt was US$12.9 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 1.65x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$12.9 billion, or 2.9% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 0.7% of GDP, equivalent to -US$3.0 billion. That deficit is the main flow-side pressure because it implies that the economy's external payments exceeded external receipts on the current-account measure provided in the packet. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$21.4 billion, while the current-account balance is -US$3.0 billion. Imports of US$73.4 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$12.9 billion adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover fell from 7.9 months to 3.5 months. This points to a weaker position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 3.4 months to 8.8 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt fell from 3.1x to 1.6x. This points to a weaker position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 1.5x to 3.9x. The current-account balance rose from -2.0% to -0.7%. This points to a stronger position. The current account ranged from -3.8% to 0.3% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap rose from -1.4% of GDP to 2.9% of GDP. This points to some tightening of the liquidity position because a higher gap is less comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -4.6% of GDP to 5.7% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 20.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 54 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is low vulnerability, indicating that the composite measure does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 50.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

The latest vulnerability score is 20.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 51 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is low vulnerability, indicating that the composite measure does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 50.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.

<!-- COUNTRY: BOL | Bolivia -->

# Bolivia

## Summary Assessment

Bolivia is classified in the packet as a Lower middle income economy in Latin America & Caribbean, with the latest observation in 2024. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 50.0 and a moderate status. The latest reserve position is US$2.0 billion, providing 1.7 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 3.11x. The current account shows a deficit of 2.4% of GDP, equal to -US$1.3 billion. The reported baseline liquidity gap is 3.5% of GDP, or US$1.9 billion. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$2.0 billion, compared with annual imports of US$14.0 billion and GDP of US$54.9 billion. Import cover stood at 1.7 months. On the packet's scoring framework, the import-cover position carries a risk score of 100.0, indicating that the stock of reserves is not fully comfortable against the import bill. Short-term external debt was US$635.4 million, and reserves covered that stock by 3.11x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$1.9 billion, or 3.5% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 2.4% of GDP, equivalent to -US$1.3 billion. That deficit is the main flow-side pressure because it implies that the economy's external payments exceeded external receipts on the current-account measure provided in the packet. The current-account component carries a risk score of 50.0, making it an important contributor to the overall dashboard reading. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$2.0 billion, while the current-account balance is -US$1.3 billion. Imports of US$14.0 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$635.4 million adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover fell from 10.3 months to 1.7 months. This points to a weaker position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 1.5 months to 10.3 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt fell from 38.0x to 3.1x. This points to a weaker position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 2.3x to 38.0x. The current-account balance rose from -4.1% to -2.4%. This points to a stronger position. The current account ranged from -4.1% to 3.3% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap rose from -11.1% of GDP to 3.5% of GDP. This points to some tightening of the liquidity position because a higher gap is less comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -11.1% of GDP to 3.8% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 50.0, with a moderate status and a vulnerability rank of 19 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is moderate vulnerability, placing the country between the low-risk group and the most pressured cases in the sample. The component scores are 100.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 50.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.

# Brazil

## Summary Assessment

The current account shows a deficit of 3.0% of GDP, equal to -US$66.2 billion. The reported baseline liquidity gap is -3.8% of GDP, or -US$82.9 billion. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$329.7 billion, compared with annual imports of US$385.7 billion and GDP of US$2185.8 billion. Import cover stood at 10.3 months. Short-term external debt was US$84.3 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 3.91x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at -US$82.9 billion, or -3.8% of GDP, which indicates a reserve cushion under the packet's baseline liquidity metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

The current account ranged from -3.4% to -1.2% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap rose from -11.4% of GDP to -3.8% of GDP. This points to some tightening of the liquidity position because a higher gap is less comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -13.8% of GDP to -3.8% of GDP.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 1987 and ends in 1994; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The latest observation is 1994, so the available public-data indicators are incomplete for assessing developments after that year. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.

<!-- COUNTRY: CHN | China -->

# China

## Summary Assessment

China is classified in the packet as an Upper middle income economy in East Asia & Pacific, with the latest observation in 2024. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 0.0 and a low status. The latest reserve position is US$3456.0 billion, providing 12.9 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 2.65x. The current account shows a surplus of 2.3% of GDP, equal to US$423.9 billion. The reported baseline liquidity gap is -7.2% of GDP, or -US$1345.3 billion. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$3456.0 billion, compared with annual imports of US$3219.3 billion and GDP of US$18743.8 billion. Import cover stood at 12.9 months. On the packet's scoring framework, this import-cover position contributes no risk to the overall vulnerability score. Short-term external debt was US$1305.9 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 2.65x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at -US$1345.3 billion, or -7.2% of GDP, which indicates a reserve cushion under the packet's baseline liquidity metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a surplus of 2.3% of GDP, equivalent to US$423.9 billion. That surplus is supportive for external liquidity because it adds a flow-side source of foreign exchange rather than creating an immediate financing need on this measure. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$3456.0 billion, while the current-account balance is US$423.9 billion. Imports of US$3219.3 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$1305.9 billion adds a stock-related liquidity test. The negative baseline gap indicates that reserves exceed the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover fell from 17.6 months to 12.9 months. This points to a weaker position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 12.6 months to 17.6 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt fell from 3.1x to 2.6x. This points to a weaker position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 2.5x to 3.1x. The current-account balance rose from 1.5% to 2.3%. This points to a stronger position. The current account ranged from 0.2% to 2.4% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap rose from -13.2% of GDP to -7.2% of GDP. This points to some tightening of the liquidity position because a higher gap is less comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -13.2% of GDP to -6.9% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 0.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 83 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is low vulnerability, indicating that the composite measure does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.

# Cameroon

## Summary Assessment

Cameroon is classified in the packet as a Lower middle income economy in Sub-Saharan Africa, with the latest observation in 2024. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 10.0 and a low status. The latest reserve position is US$5.1 billion, providing 6.1 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 5.32x. The current account shows a deficit of 3.3% of GDP, equal to -US$1.8 billion. The reported baseline liquidity gap is 0.3% of GDP, or US$135.0 million. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$5.1 billion, compared with annual imports of US$10.0 billion and GDP of US$53.3 billion. Import cover stood at 6.1 months. On the packet's scoring framework, this import-cover position contributes no risk to the overall vulnerability score. Short-term external debt was US$950.9 million, and reserves covered that stock by 5.32x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$135.0 million, or 0.3% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 3.3% of GDP, equivalent to -US$1.8 billion. That deficit is the main flow-side pressure because it implies that the economy's external payments exceeded external receipts on the current-account measure provided in the packet. The current-account component carries a risk score of 50.0, making it an important contributor to the overall dashboard reading. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$5.1 billion, while the current-account balance is -US$1.8 billion. Imports of US$10.0 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$950.9 million adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover rose from 5.1 months to 6.1 months. This points to a stronger position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 4.8 months to 6.3 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt fell from 6.1x to 5.3x. This points to a weaker position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 5.3x to 10.8x. The current-account balance fell from -2.6% to -3.3%. This points to a weaker position. The current account ranged from -4.3% to -2.6% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap fell from 0.4% of GDP to 0.3% of GDP. This points to some easing of pressure because a lower gap is more comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -0.7% of GDP to 1.7% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 10.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 61 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is low vulnerability, indicating that the composite measure does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 50.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. # Comoros

## Summary Assessment

Comoros is classified in the packet as a Lower middle income economy in Sub-Saharan Africa, with the latest observation in 2023. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 0.0 and a low status. The latest reserve position is US$324.6 million, providing 8.3 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 715.81x. The current account shows a deficit of 1.9% of GDP, equal to -US$24.6 million. The reported baseline liquidity gap is -13.7% of GDP, or -US$182.3 million. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2023, reported reserves were US$324.6 million, compared with annual imports of US$468.8 million and GDP of US$1.3 billion. Import cover stood at 8.3 months. On the packet's scoring framework, this import-cover position contributes no risk to the overall vulnerability score. Short-term external debt was US$453,417, and reserves covered that stock by 715.81x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at -US$182.3 million, or -13.7% of GDP, which indicates a reserve cushion under the packet's baseline liquidity metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 1.9% of GDP, equivalent to -US$24.6 million. That deficit is the main flow-side pressure because it implies that the economy's external payments exceeded external receipts on the current-account measure provided in the packet. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$324.6 million, while the current-account balance is -US$24.6 million. Imports of US$468.8 million create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$453,417 adds a stock-related liquidity test. The negative baseline gap indicates that reserves exceed the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2016-2023. Import cover rose from 7.1 months to 8.3 months. This points to a stronger position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 6.7 months to 10.3 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt rose from 105.1x to 715.8x. This points to a stronger position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 105.1x to 715.8x. The current-account balance rose from -4.3% to -1.9%. This points to a stronger position. The current account ranged from -4.3% to -0.3% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap fell from -4.6% of GDP to -13.7% of GDP. This points to some easing of pressure because a lower gap is more comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -17.4% of GDP to -4.6% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 0.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 77 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is low vulnerability, indicating that the composite measure does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2016 and ends in 2023; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The latest observation is 2023, so the available public-data indicators are incomplete for assessing developments after that year. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.
