# Cabo Verde

## Summary Assessment

Cabo Verde is classified in the packet as an Upper middle income economy in Sub-Saharan Africa, with the latest observation in 2024. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 0.0 and a low status. The latest reserve position is US$783.1 million, providing 6.4 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 2.02x. The current account shows a surplus of 3.9% of GDP, equal to US$106.6 million. The reported baseline liquidity gap is -1.0% of GDP, or -US$27.0 million. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$783.1 million, compared with annual imports of US$1.5 billion and GDP of US$2.7 billion. Import cover stood at 6.4 months. On the packet's scoring framework, this import-cover position contributes no risk to the overall vulnerability score. Short-term external debt was US$388.1 million, and reserves covered that stock by 2.02x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at -US$27.0 million, or -1.0% of GDP, which indicates a reserve cushion under the packet's baseline liquidity metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a surplus of 3.9% of GDP, equivalent to US$106.6 million. That surplus is supportive for external liquidity because it adds a flow-side source of foreign exchange rather than creating an immediate financing need on this measure. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$783.1 million, while the current-account balance is US$106.6 million. Imports of US$1.5 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$388.1 million adds a stock-related liquidity test. The negative baseline gap indicates that reserves exceed the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover was unchanged from 6.4 months to 6.4 months. Across the series, import cover ranged from 5.5 months to 9.1 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt rose from 1.9x to 2.0x. This points to a stronger position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 1.8x to 2.3x. The current-account balance rose from -7.0% to 3.9%. This points to a stronger position. The current account ranged from -15.7% to 3.9% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap fell from 6.8% of GDP to -1.0% of GDP. This points to some easing of pressure because a lower gap is more comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -3.1% of GDP to 7.8% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 0.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 80 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is low vulnerability, indicating that the composite measure does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.

# Djibouti

## Summary Assessment

Djibouti is classified in the packet as a Lower middle income economy in Middle East, North Africa, Afghanistan & Pakistan, with the latest observation in 2024. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 80.0 and a high status. The latest reserve position is US$348.7 million, providing 0.9 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 0.67x. The current account shows a surplus of 14.7% of GDP, equal to US$610.1 million. The reported baseline liquidity gap is 12.5% of GDP, or US$518.1 million. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$348.7 million, compared with annual imports of US$4.8 billion and GDP of US$4.2 billion. Import cover stood at 0.9 months. On the packet's scoring framework, the import-cover position carries a risk score of 100.0, indicating that the stock of reserves is not fully comfortable against the import bill. Short-term external debt was US$518.1 million, and reserves covered that stock by 0.67x. The short-term-debt coverage component has a risk score of 100.0, so debt-service concentration at the short end remains a relevant liquidity issue in the dashboard. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$518.1 million, or 12.5% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a surplus of 14.7% of GDP, equivalent to US$610.1 million. That surplus is supportive for external liquidity because it adds a flow-side source of foreign exchange rather than creating an immediate financing need on this measure. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$348.7 million, while the current-account balance is US$610.1 million. Imports of US$4.8 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$518.1 million adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover fell from 1.5 months to 0.9 months. This points to a weaker position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 0.9 months to 2.4 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt fell from 2.0x to 0.7x. This points to a weaker position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 0.7x to 2.7x. The current-account balance rose from -4.8% to 14.7%. This points to a stronger position. The current account ranged from -6.6% to 18.5% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap fell from 14.9% of GDP to 12.5% of GDP. This points to some easing of pressure because a lower gap is more comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from 8.1% of GDP to 26.4% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 80.0, with a high status and a vulnerability rank of 6 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is high vulnerability, so the country sits in the more pressured segment of the sample on the packet's composite measure. The component scores are 100.0 for import-cover risk, 100.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

# Dominican Republic

## Summary Assessment

Dominican Republic is classified in the packet as an Upper middle income economy in Latin America & Caribbean, with the latest observation in 2024. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 30.0 and a moderate status. The latest reserve position is US$13.5 billion, providing 4.5 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 3.25x. The current account shows a deficit of 3.4% of GDP, equal to -US$4.2 billion. The reported baseline liquidity gap is 3.1% of GDP, or US$3.9 billion. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$13.5 billion, compared with annual imports of US$36.1 billion and GDP of US$124.3 billion. Import cover stood at 4.5 months. On the packet's scoring framework, the import-cover position carries a risk score of 50.0, indicating that the stock of reserves is not fully comfortable against the import bill. Short-term external debt was US$4.1 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 3.25x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$3.9 billion, or 3.1% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 3.4% of GDP, equivalent to -US$4.2 billion. That deficit is the main flow-side pressure because it implies that the economy's external payments exceeded external receipts on the current-account measure provided in the packet. The current-account component carries a risk score of 50.0, making it an important contributor to the overall dashboard reading. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$13.5 billion, while the current-account balance is -US$4.2 billion. Imports of US$36.1 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$4.1 billion adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover rose from 3.9 months to 4.5 months. This points to a stronger position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 3.8 months to 6.4 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt fell from 3.4x to 3.2x. This points to a weaker position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 3.2x to 5.7x. The current-account balance fell from -0.2% to -3.4%. This points to a weaker position. The current account ranged from -5.8% to -0.2% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap rose from 0.7% of GDP to 3.1% of GDP. This points to some tightening of the liquidity position because a higher gap is less comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -2.9% of GDP to 3.9% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 30.0, with a moderate status and a vulnerability rank of 39 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is moderate vulnerability, placing the country between the low-risk group and the most pressured cases in the sample. The component scores are 50.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 50.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. That surplus is supportive for external liquidity because it adds a flow-side source of foreign exchange rather than creating an immediate financing need on this measure. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$6.9 billion, while the current-account balance is US$7.0 billion. Imports of US$33.6 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$626.2 million adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover rose from 1.1 months to 2.5 months. This points to a stronger position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 1.0 months to 4.3 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt rose from 2.0x to 11.0x. This points to a stronger position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 2.0x to 11.0x. The current-account balance rose from -0.4% to 5.7%. This points to a stronger position. The current account ranged from -1.5% to 5.7% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap fell from 1.4% of GDP to 0.5% of GDP. This points to some easing of pressure because a lower gap is more comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -1.1% of GDP to 2.5% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 40.0, with a moderate status and a vulnerability rank of 27 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is moderate vulnerability, placing the country between the low-risk group and the most pressured cases in the sample. The component scores are 100.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.

# Egypt, Arab Rep.

## Summary Assessment

Egypt, Arab Rep. is classified in the packet as a Lower middle income economy in Middle East, North Africa, Afghanistan & Pakistan, with the latest observation in 2024. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 20.0 and a low status. The latest reserve position is US$44.9 billion, providing 6.0 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 1.45x. The current account shows a deficit of 5.7% of GDP, equal to -US$22.1 billion. The reported baseline liquidity gap is 7.9% of GDP, or US$30.8 billion. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$44.9 billion, compared with annual imports of US$90.4 billion and GDP of US$389.1 billion. Import cover stood at 6.0 months. On the packet's scoring framework, this import-cover position contributes no risk to the overall vulnerability score. Short-term external debt was US$31.0 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 1.45x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$30.8 billion, or 7.9% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 5.7% of GDP, equivalent to -US$22.1 billion. That deficit is the main flow-side pressure because it implies that the economy's external payments exceeded external receipts on the current-account measure provided in the packet. The current-account component carries a risk score of 100.0, making it an important contributor to the overall dashboard reading. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$44.9 billion, while the current-account balance is -US$22.1 billion. Imports of US$90.4 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$31.0 billion adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover fell from 6.3 months to 6.0 months. This points to a weaker position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 3.7 months to 6.9 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt fell from 3.3x to 1.4x. This points to a weaker position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 1.1x to 4.0x. The current-account balance fell from -3.2% to -5.7%. This points to a weaker position. The current account ranged from -5.7% to -2.2% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap rose from -0.0% of GDP to 7.9% of GDP. This points to some tightening of the liquidity position because a higher gap is less comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -2.1% of GDP to 7.9% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 20.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 52 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is low vulnerability, indicating that the composite measure does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 100.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.

# Eritrea

The current account shows a deficit of 14.8% of GDP, equal to -US$104.7 million. The reported baseline liquidity gap is 16.5% of GDP, or US$116.6 million. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2000, reported reserves were US$36.1 million, compared with annual imports of US$411.2 million and GDP of US$706.4 million. Import cover stood at 1.1 months. On the packet's scoring framework, the import-cover position carries a risk score of 100.0, indicating that the stock of reserves is not fully comfortable against the import bill. Short-term external debt was US$12.0 million, and reserves covered that stock by 3.02x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$116.6 million, or 16.5% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 14.8% of GDP, equivalent to -US$104.7 million. That deficit is the main flow-side pressure because it implies that the economy's external payments exceeded external receipts on the current-account measure provided in the packet. The current-account component carries a risk score of 100.0, making it an important contributor to the overall dashboard reading. Imports of US$411.2 million create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$12.0 million adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

Reserve coverage of short-term external debt fell from infx to 3.0x. This points to a weaker position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 3.0x to infx. The current-account balance fell from -5.4% to -14.8%. This points to a weaker position. The current account ranged from -39.3% to 0.7% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap rose from 5.4% of GDP to 16.5% of GDP. This points to some tightening of the liquidity position because a higher gap is less comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -12.0% of GDP to 39.3% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 60.0, with a high status and a vulnerability rank of 11 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is high vulnerability, so the country sits in the more pressured segment of the sample on the packet's composite measure. The component scores are 100.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 100.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

The latest observation is 2000, so the available public-data indicators are incomplete for assessing developments after that year. This points to some tightening of the liquidity position because a higher gap is less comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from 13.8% of GDP to 19.1% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 100.0, with a high status and a vulnerability rank of 1 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is high vulnerability, so the country sits in the more pressured segment of the sample on the packet's composite measure. The component scores are 100.0 for import-cover risk, 100.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 100.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.

<!-- COUNTRY: GHA | Ghana -->

# Ghana

## Summary Assessment

Ghana is classified in the packet as a Lower middle income economy in Sub-Saharan Africa, with the latest observation in 2024. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 40.0 and a moderate status. The latest reserve position is US$4.5 billion, providing 1.9 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 1.71x. The current account shows a surplus of 2.0% of GDP, equal to US$1.7 billion. The reported baseline liquidity gap is 3.2% of GDP, or US$2.6 billion. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$4.5 billion, compared with annual imports of US$28.2 billion and GDP of US$82.3 billion. Import cover stood at 1.9 months. On the packet's scoring framework, the import-cover position carries a risk score of 100.0, indicating that the stock of reserves is not fully comfortable against the import bill. Short-term external debt was US$2.6 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 1.71x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$2.6 billion, or 3.2% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a surplus of 2.0% of GDP, equivalent to US$1.7 billion. That surplus is supportive for external liquidity because it adds a flow-side source of foreign exchange rather than creating an immediate financing need on this measure. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$4.5 billion, while the current-account balance is US$1.7 billion. Imports of US$28.2 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$2.6 billion adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover fell from 3.8 months to 1.9 months. This points to a weaker position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 1.7 months to 4.6 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt fell from 2.1x to 1.7x. This points to a weaker position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 1.0x to 2.1x. The current-account balance rose from -3.3% to 2.0%. This points to a stronger position. The current account ranged from -3.3% to 2.0% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap fell from 6.4% of GDP to 3.2% of GDP. This points to some easing of pressure because a lower gap is more comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from 3.2% of GDP to 8.6% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 40.0, with a moderate status and a vulnerability rank of 30 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is moderate vulnerability, placing the country between the low-risk group and the most pressured cases in the sample. The component scores are 100.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.

# Gambia, The

## Summary Assessment

The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 10.0 and a low status. The latest reserve position is US$628.8 million, providing 9.4 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 10.22x. The current account shows a deficit of 4.1% of GDP, equal to -US$99.8 million. The reported baseline liquidity gap is -11.1% of GDP, or -US$266.6 million. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$628.8 million, compared with annual imports of US$803.8 million and GDP of US$2.4 billion. Import cover stood at 9.4 months. On the packet's scoring framework, this import-cover position contributes no risk to the overall vulnerability score. Short-term external debt was US$61.6 million, and reserves covered that stock by 10.22x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at -US$266.6 million, or -11.1% of GDP, which indicates a reserve cushion under the packet's baseline liquidity metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 4.1% of GDP, equivalent to -US$99.8 million. That deficit is the main flow-side pressure because it implies that the economy's external payments exceeded external receipts on the current-account measure provided in the packet. The current-account component carries a risk score of 50.0, making it an important contributor to the overall dashboard reading. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$628.8 million, while the current-account balance is -US$99.8 million. Imports of US$803.8 million create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$61.6 million adds a stock-related liquidity test. The negative baseline gap indicates that reserves exceed the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover rose from 3.6 months to 9.4 months. This points to a stronger position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 3.2 months to 11.0 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt fell from 11.8x to 10.2x. This points to a weaker position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 6.3x to 17.2x. The current-account balance rose from -6.3% to -4.1%. This points to a stronger position. The current account ranged from -8.1% to -2.0% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap fell from 5.3% of GDP to -11.1% of GDP. This points to some easing of pressure because a lower gap is more comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -17.3% of GDP to 8.4% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 10.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 64 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is low vulnerability, indicating that the composite measure does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 50.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 1998 and ends in 2005; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The latest observation is 2005, so the available public-data indicators are incomplete for assessing developments after that year. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.

<!-- COUNTRY: HND | Honduras -->

# Honduras

## Summary Assessment

Honduras is classified in the packet as a Lower middle income economy in Latin America & Caribbean, with the latest observation in 2024. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 30.0 and a moderate status. The latest reserve position is US$8.0 billion, providing 4.5 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 4.56x. The current account shows a deficit of 4.5% of GDP, equal to -US$1.7 billion. The reported baseline liquidity gap is 1.9% of GDP, or US$714.7 million. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$8.0 billion, compared with annual imports of US$21.4 billion and GDP of US$37.1 billion. Import cover stood at 4.5 months. On the packet's scoring framework, the import-cover position carries a risk score of 50.0, indicating that the stock of reserves is not fully comfortable against the import bill. Short-term external debt was US$1.8 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 4.56x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$714.7 million, or 1.9% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 4.5% of GDP, equivalent to -US$1.7 billion. That deficit is the main flow-side pressure because it implies that the economy's external payments exceeded external receipts on the current-account measure provided in the packet. The current-account component carries a risk score of 50.0, making it an important contributor to the overall dashboard reading. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$8.0 billion, while the current-account balance is -US$1.7 billion. Imports of US$21.4 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$1.8 billion adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover rose from 4.2 months to 4.5 months. This points to a stronger position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 3.8 months to 8.0 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt fell from 9.6x to 4.6x. This points to a weaker position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 4.6x to 13.0x. The current-account balance fell from -1.2% to -4.5%. This points to a weaker position. The current account ranged from -6.9% to 2.9% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap rose from -2.3% of GDP to 1.9% of GDP. This points to some tightening of the liquidity position because a higher gap is less comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -19.1% of GDP to 5.2% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 30.0, with a moderate status and a vulnerability rank of 38 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is moderate vulnerability, placing the country between the low-risk group and the most pressured cases in the sample. The component scores are 50.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 50.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.

The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 0.0 and a low status. The latest reserve position is US$100.7 billion, providing 13.0 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 138.66x. The current account shows a surplus of 3.0% of GDP, equal to US$8.4 billion. The reported baseline liquidity gap is -27.4% of GDP, or -US$76.8 billion. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$100.7 billion, compared with annual imports of US$92.8 billion and GDP of US$279.6 billion. Import cover stood at 13.0 months. On the packet's scoring framework, this import-cover position contributes no risk to the overall vulnerability score. Short-term external debt was US$726.2 million, and reserves covered that stock by 138.66x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at -US$76.8 billion, or -27.4% of GDP, which indicates a reserve cushion under the packet's baseline liquidity metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a surplus of 3.0% of GDP, equivalent to US$8.4 billion. That surplus is supportive for external liquidity because it adds a flow-side source of foreign exchange rather than creating an immediate financing need on this measure. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$100.7 billion, while the current-account balance is US$8.4 billion. Imports of US$92.8 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$726.2 million adds a stock-related liquidity test. The negative baseline gap indicates that reserves exceed the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover rose from 12.2 months to 13.0 months. This points to a stronger position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 11.3 months to 16.8 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt rose from 45.2x to 138.7x. This points to a stronger position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 45.2x to 138.7x. The current-account balance fell from 8.0% to 3.0%. This points to a weaker position. The current account ranged from -3.5% to 20.2% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap fell from -19.3% of GDP to -27.4% of GDP. This points to some easing of pressure because a lower gap is more comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -33.9% of GDP to -18.8% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 0.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 92 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is low vulnerability, indicating that the composite measure does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

Similarly, the reserves-to-short-term-external-debt ratio is a useful rollover-risk indicator, but it does not show the creditor structure, currency composition, repayment calendar, or whether short-term liabilities are public, private, or financial-sector obligations. The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. For that reason, the note confines itself to the supplied indicators and should be used as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign credit or balance-of-payments assessment.

# Jordan

## Summary Assessment

Jordan is classified in the packet as a Lower middle income economy in Middle East, North Africa, Afghanistan & Pakistan, with the latest observation in 2024. The sovereign external-liquidity dashboard assigns a vulnerability score of 20.0 and a low status. The latest reserve position is US$21.9 billion, providing 8.6 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 1.23x. The current account shows a deficit of 5.9% of GDP, equal to -US$3.1 billion. The reported baseline liquidity gap is 12.6% of GDP, or US$6.7 billion. The overall message is that the dashboard signal should be read through three channels: the reserve stock relative to imports, the reserve stock relative to short-term external debt, and the direction and size of the current-account balance.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$21.9 billion, compared with annual imports of US$30.4 billion and GDP of US$53.4 billion. Import cover stood at 8.6 months. On the packet's scoring framework, this import-cover position contributes no risk to the overall vulnerability score. Short-term external debt was US$17.9 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 1.23x. The short-term-debt coverage component also contributes no risk in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$6.7 billion, or 12.6% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 5.9% of GDP, equivalent to -US$3.1 billion. That deficit is the main flow-side pressure because it implies that the economy's external payments exceeded external receipts on the current-account measure provided in the packet. The current-account component carries a risk score of 100.0, making it an important contributor to the overall dashboard reading. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$21.9 billion, while the current-account balance is -US$3.1 billion. Imports of US$30.4 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$17.9 billion adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. This makes the latest reading a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage rather than a single-indicator judgment.

## Trend Interpretation

The recent-history series covers 2017-2024. Import cover rose from 8.1 months to 8.6 months. This points to a stronger position. Across the series, import cover ranged from 7.3 months to 11.0 months. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt fell from 1.4x to 1.2x. This points to a weaker position. The reserves-to-short-term-debt ratio ranged from 1.2x to 1.4x. The current-account balance rose from -10.6% to -5.9%. This points to a stronger position. The current account ranged from -10.6% to -1.7% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap fell from 13.3% of GDP to 12.6% of GDP. This points to some easing of pressure because a lower gap is more comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from 7.1% of GDP to 18.4% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 20.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 49 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is low vulnerability, indicating that the composite measure does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 100.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, none of which are provided in the packet. The composite result is therefore useful for cross-country screening, while the underlying indicators are needed to identify whether the signal is being driven by import coverage, short-term-debt coverage, or the current-account balance.

For surveillance purposes, the interpretation should remain indicator-led. A comfortable reserve-to-import ratio can coexist with pressure from a weak current account, while a current-account surplus can coexist with a need to monitor the maturity profile of external liabilities. The packet's component scores help separate these channels and prevent the composite score from being read as a single undifferentiated risk signal.

The baseline liquidity gap is especially useful because it combines the stock of reserves with other near-term external-liquidity needs. A negative value should not be interpreted as the absence of all external risk; it means only that the reserve stock exceeds the baseline requirement embedded in the packet. A positive value is more concerning because it identifies a shortfall against that same metric.

The import-cover measure should also be read with caution. It captures the number of months of imports that could be financed by the reserve stock, but it does not reveal import compression, terms-of-trade movements, seasonality, or the share of imports that is essential. The packet therefore supports a reserve-adequacy screen, not a granular import-demand diagnosis.

The latest ratio should therefore be interpreted as a coverage metric within the packet's defined scope.

The packet does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. The current account ranged from -42.7% to 4.5% of GDP, showing the external-flow variation visible in the packet. The baseline liquidity gap rose from 13.3% of GDP to 49.5% of GDP. This points to some tightening of the liquidity position because a higher gap is less comfortable on this metric. The gap metric ranged from -15.3% of GDP to 49.5% of GDP.

## Relative Vulnerability

The dashboard classification is moderate vulnerability, placing the country between the low-risk group and the most pressured cases in the sample. The component scores are 50.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 100.0 for current-account risk.
